<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Sea level rise</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the current and future rise in sea level associated with <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>. For general discussion of sea level, see <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">Sea level</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">The current long-term trend for sea levels to rise mainly in response to global warming.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Evolution-of-GMSL-time-series-from-six-different-groups%E2%80%99.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Evolution-of-GMSL-time-series-from-six-different-groups%E2%80%99.jpg/400px-Evolution-of-GMSL-time-series-from-six-different-groups%E2%80%99.jpg" width="400" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Evolution-of-GMSL-time-series-from-six-different-groups%E2%80%99.jpg/600px-Evolution-of-GMSL-time-series-from-six-different-groups%E2%80%99.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Evolution-of-GMSL-time-series-from-six-different-groups%E2%80%99.jpg/800px-Evolution-of-GMSL-time-series-from-six-different-groups%E2%80%99.jpg 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="639" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Evolution-of-GMSL-time-series-from-six-different-groups%E2%80%99.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global mean sea level evolution from different datasets (1993–2018).</div></div></div>
<p>At least since 1880, the average global <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a> has been rising. This is due mostly to anthropogenic <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> that is driving the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a> of seawater while melting land-based <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">ice sheets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mengel_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mengel-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>  This trend is expected to accelerate during the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>62</span></sup>
</p><p>Projecting future sea level has always been challenging, due to our imperfect understanding of many aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Climate_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate system">climate system</a>.  As <a href="/wiki/Climatology" title="Climatology">climate research</a> leads to improved <a href="/wiki/Climate_model" title="Climate model">computer models</a>, projections have consistently increased.  For example, in 2007 the high end of <a href="/wiki/IPCC" class="mw-redirect" title="IPCC">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) projections through 2099 was less than 2 feet (0.61&#160;m),<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> but in their 2014 report the high end was considered to be about 3 feet (0.91&#160;m).<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> A number of later studies have concluded that 2.0 to 2.7 metres (6&#160;ft 7&#160;in to 8&#160;ft 10&#160;in) rise this century is "physically plausible".<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The contributions to <b>sea level rise</b> since 1993, based on 2018 figures, divide into ocean <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a> (42%), melting of temperate glaciers (21%), <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> (15%) and <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> (8%).<sup id="cite_ref-WCRP2018_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCRP2018-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sea level rise will not be the same at every location on earth, with some locations even getting a drop in sea levels. Local factors include <a href="/wiki/Tectonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Tectonic">tectonic</a> effects, and <a href="/wiki/Subsidence" title="Subsidence">subsidence</a> of the land, tides, currents and storms. Sea level rise is expected to continue for centuries. Because of <a href="/wiki/Climate_inertia" title="Climate inertia">long response times</a> for parts of the climate system, it has been estimated that we are committed to a sea-level rise of approximately 2.3 metres (7.5&#160;ft) for each <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">Celsius</a> degree of temperature rise within the next 2,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sea level rises <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_humans" class="mw-redirect" title="Effects of climate change on humans">can considerably influence human populations</a> in coastal and island regions and natural environments like <a href="/wiki/Marine_ecosystem" title="Marine ecosystem">marine ecosystems</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wg1-5-5-1_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wg1-5-5-1-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Widespread <a href="/wiki/Coastal_flood" title="Coastal flood">coastal flooding</a> would be expected if several degrees of warming is sustained for millennia.<sup id="cite_ref-us_nrc_2011_long_term_slr_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_2011_long_term_slr-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> For example, sustained global warming of more than 2&#160;C° relative to pre-industrial levels could lead to eventual sea level rise of about 1–4 metres (3.3–13&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-us_nrc_2011_long_term_slr_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_2011_long_term_slr-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Societies can respond to sea level rise in three different ways: retreat, accommodate and protect. Sometimes these adaptation strategies go hand in hand, but at other times choices have to be made between different strategies. Ecosystems that adapt to rising sea levels by moving inland might not always be able to do so, due to natural or man made barriers.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Past_changes_in_sea_level">Past changes in sea level</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Past changes in sea level">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Post-Glacial_Sea_Level.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Post-Glacial_Sea_Level.png/220px-Post-Glacial_Sea_Level.png" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Post-Glacial_Sea_Level.png/330px-Post-Glacial_Sea_Level.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Post-Glacial_Sea_Level.png/440px-Post-Glacial_Sea_Level.png 2x" data-file-width="526" data-file-height="359" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Post-Glacial_Sea_Level.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Changes in sea level since the end of the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial episode</a></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Past_sea_level" title="Past sea level">Past sea level</a></div><p>Understanding past sea level is important for the analysis of current and future changes. In the recent geological past changes in land ice and thermal expansion from increased temperatures are the dominant reasons of sea level rise. The last time the Earth was 2&#160;°C warmer than the pre-industrial temperatures, sea levels were at least 5 metres (16&#160;ft) higher than present. This was during the last <a href="/wiki/Interglacial" title="Interglacial">interglacial</a>, when the earth warming was caused by slow changes in the <a href="/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles" title="Milankovitch cycles">orbital forcing</a>. The warming was sustained over a period of thousands of years and the magnitude of the rise in sea level implied a large contribution from the Antarctic and Greenland ice sheets.<sup id="cite_ref-ipcc13_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc13-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1139</span></sup>
</p><p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">last glacial maximum</a> about 20,000 years ago, the sea level has risen by more than 125 metres (410&#160;ft), with rates varying from less than a mm/year to 40+ mm/year, as a result of melting ice sheets over Canada and Eurasia. Rapid disintegration of ice sheets led to so called '<a href="/wiki/Meltwater_pulse_1A" title="Meltwater pulse 1A">meltwater pulses</a>', periods during which sea level rose rapidly. The rate of rise started to slow down 8.2 thousand years before present; the sea level was almost constant in the last 2,500 years, before the recent rising trend starting approximately in 1850.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_level_measurement">Sea level measurement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Sea level measurement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>To get precise measurements for sea level, researchers studying the state of frozen water and the ocean on our planet factor in ongoing <a href="/wiki/Geomorphology" title="Geomorphology">deformations of the solid Earth</a>, in particular due to <a href="/wiki/Glacial_isostatic_adjustment" class="mw-redirect" title="Glacial isostatic adjustment">landmasses still rising from past ice masses retreating</a>. Additionally, <a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">Earth gravitation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth&#39;s rotation">rotation</a> have to be accounted for. These factors are dependent on the different layers which make up the Earth (<a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asthenosphere" title="Asthenosphere">asthenosphere</a>), and the order in which land-based ice melts. Because the involved processes, which are collectively known as the <i>Sea-level equation</i>, change very slowly, on time scales of thousands of years, they are considered to be constant.<sup id="cite_ref-WCRP2018_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCRP2018-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Satellites">Satellites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Satellites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TOPEX-JasonSeries2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TOPEX-JasonSeries2008.jpg/220px-TOPEX-JasonSeries2008.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TOPEX-JasonSeries2008.jpg/330px-TOPEX-JasonSeries2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TOPEX-JasonSeries2008.jpg/440px-TOPEX-JasonSeries2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TOPEX-JasonSeries2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jason-1" title="Jason-1">Jason-1</a> continued the sea surface measurements started by TOPEX/Poseidon. It was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Ocean_Surface_Topography_Mission" title="Ocean Surface Topography Mission">Ocean Surface Topography Mission</a> on <a href="/wiki/Jason-2" class="mw-redirect" title="Jason-2">Jason-2</a>, and by <a href="/wiki/Jason-3" title="Jason-3">Jason-3</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NASA-Satellite-sea-level-rise-observations-1993-April-2018.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/NASA-Satellite-sea-level-rise-observations-1993-April-2018.jpg/220px-NASA-Satellite-sea-level-rise-observations-1993-April-2018.jpg" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/NASA-Satellite-sea-level-rise-observations-1993-April-2018.jpg/330px-NASA-Satellite-sea-level-rise-observations-1993-April-2018.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/NASA-Satellite-sea-level-rise-observations-1993-April-2018.jpg/440px-NASA-Satellite-sea-level-rise-observations-1993-April-2018.jpg 2x" data-file-width="653" data-file-height="404" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NASA-Satellite-sea-level-rise-observations-1993-April-2018.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Satellite measurements of sea level, in millimeters, 1993–2018 (April).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NOAA_sea_level_trend_1993_2010.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/NOAA_sea_level_trend_1993_2010.png/220px-NOAA_sea_level_trend_1993_2010.png" width="220" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/NOAA_sea_level_trend_1993_2010.png/330px-NOAA_sea_level_trend_1993_2010.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/NOAA_sea_level_trend_1993_2010.png/440px-NOAA_sea_level_trend_1993_2010.png 2x" data-file-width="2425" data-file-height="1337" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NOAA_sea_level_trend_1993_2010.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sea level trends from <a href="/wiki/Satellite_altimetry" class="mw-redirect" title="Satellite altimetry">satellite altimetry</a> (1993–2012)</div></div></div>
<p>Since the 1992 launch of <a href="/wiki/TOPEX/Poseidon" title="TOPEX/Poseidon">TOPEX/Poseidon</a>, altimetric satellites have been recording the change in sea level.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Those satellites can measure the hills and valleys in the sea caused by currents and detect trends in their height. To measure the distance to the sea surface, the satellite sends a microwave pulse to the ocean's surface and record the time it takes to return. A <a href="/wiki/Microwave_radiometer" title="Microwave radiometer">microwave radiometer</a> corrects any delay that may be caused by <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>. Combining this data with the precise location of the spacecraft makes it possible to determine sea-surface height to within a few centimeters (about one inch).<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Current rates of sea level rise from <a href="/wiki/Satellite_altimetry" class="mw-redirect" title="Satellite altimetry">satellite altimetry</a> have been estimated to be 3.0&#160;±&#160;0.4 millimetres (0.118&#160;±&#160;0.016&#160;in) per year for the period 1993–2017.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Earlier satellite measurements were previously at odds with <a href="/wiki/Tide_gauge" title="Tide gauge">tide gauge</a> measurements. A small calibration error for the Topex/Poseidon satellite discovered in 2015 was identified as the cause of this mismatch. It had caused a slight overestimation of the 1992–2005 sea levels, which masked the ongoing sea level rise acceleration.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With satellites it is possible to capture regional variations in sea level rise well. In the 1993–2012 period for instance, sea level rose substantially in the western tropical Pacific. The sharp rise in this area has been linked to increasing <a href="/wiki/Trade_winds" title="Trade winds">trade winds</a>, which occur when the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Decadal_Oscillation" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacific Decadal Oscillation">Pacific Decadal Oscillation</a> (PDO) and the <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o%E2%80%93Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño–Southern Oscillation">El Niño–Southern Oscillation</a> (ENSO) change from one state to the other.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The PDO is a basin-wide climate pattern consisting of two phases, each commonly lasting 10 to 30 years, while the ENSO has a shorter period of 2 to 7 years.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tide_gauges">Tide gauges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Tide gauges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Another important source of sea-level observations comes from the global network of tide gauges. In contrast to the satellite record, this record has a lot of spatial and temporal gaps.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Coverage of tide gauges started primarily in the Northern Hemisphere, with data for the Southern Hemisphere remained scarce up to the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The longest running sea-level measurements, NAP or <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam_Ordnance_Datum" title="Amsterdam Ordnance Datum">Amsterdam Ordnance Datum</a> established in 1675, are recorded in <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Netherlands" class="mw-redirect" title="The Netherlands">the Netherlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>  In Australia, record collection is also quite extensive, including measurements by an amateur meteorologist beginning in 1837 and measurements taken from a sea-level benchmark struck on a small cliff on the Isle of the Dead<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> near the <a href="/wiki/Port_Arthur,_Tasmania" title="Port Arthur, Tasmania">Port Arthur</a> convict settlement in 1841.
</p><p>This network was used, in combination with satellite altimeter data, to establish that global mean sea-level rose 19.5&#160;cm (7.7&#160;in) between 1870 and 2004 at an average rate of about 1.44&#160;mm/yr (1.7&#160;mm/yr during the 20th century).<sup id="cite_ref-church_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-church-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> This is an important confirmation of climate change simulations, predicting that SLR will accelerate in response to global warming. In <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, data collected by the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Scientific_and_Industrial_Research_Organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation">Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation</a> (CSIRO) show the current global mean sea level trend to be 3.2&#160;mm (0.13&#160;in) per year, a doubling of the rate during the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some regional differences are also visible in the tide gauge data. Some of the recorded regional differences are due to differences in the actual sea level, while other are due to vertical land movements. In the United States for instance, considerable variation is found because some land areas are rising and some are sinking. Over the past 100 years, the rate of sea level rise varied from an increase of about 0.36 inches (9.1&#160;mm) per year along the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> Coast due to land sinking, to a drop of a few inches per decade in parts of <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Post-glacial_rebound" title="Post-glacial rebound">post-glacial rebound</a>. The rate of sea level rise increased during the 1993–2003 period compared with the longer-term average (1961–2003), although it is unclear whether the faster rate reflected a short-term variation or an increase in the long-term trend.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contributions">Contributions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Contributions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet_dynamics" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice sheet dynamics">Ice sheet dynamics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Corp2400_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Corp2400_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/170px-Corp2400_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg" width="170" height="251" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Corp2400_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/255px-Corp2400_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Corp2400_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/340px-Corp2400_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1196" data-file-height="1764" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Corp2400_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Close-up of <a href="/wiki/Ross_Ice_Shelf" title="Ross Ice Shelf">Ross Ice Shelf</a>, the largest ice shelf of Antarctica, about the size of France and up to several hundred metres thick.</div></div></div>
<p>There are three main contributions to sea level rise. Oceans <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">expand</a> if they are warming, <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a> at high altitudes melt and the total mass of ice sheets decreases. Sea level rise in the last 150 years was dominated by retreat of glaciers and expansion of the ocean, but the contributions of the two large ice sheets (Greenland and Antarctica) is expected to increase in the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-Mengel_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mengel-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The ice sheets store most of the land ice (∼99.5%), with a sea-level equivalent (SLE) of 7.4&#160;m (24&#160;ft) for Greenland and 58.3&#160;m (191&#160;ft) for Antarctica.<sup id="cite_ref-WCRP2018_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCRP2018-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Each year about 8&#160;mm (0.31&#160;in) of <a href="/wiki/Precipitation#Measurement" title="Precipitation">precipitation (liquid equivalent)</a> falls on the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">ice sheets in Antarctica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet" title="Greenland ice sheet">Greenland</a>, mostly as snow, which accumulates and over time forms glacial ice. Much of this precipitation began as water vapor evaporated from the ocean surface. To a first approximation, the same amount of water appeared to return to the ocean in <a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">icebergs</a> and from ice melting at the edges. Scientists previously had estimated which is greater, ice going in or coming out, called the <a href="/wiki/Glacier_mass_balance" title="Glacier mass balance">glacier mass balance</a>, important because a nonzero balance causes changes in global sea level. The rate of ice loss is accelerating.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ocean_heating">Ocean heating</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ocean heating">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ocean_heat_content" title="Ocean heat content">Ocean heat content</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ocean_Heat_Content_(2012).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ocean_Heat_Content_%282012%29.png/220px-Ocean_Heat_Content_%282012%29.png" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ocean_Heat_Content_%282012%29.png/330px-Ocean_Heat_Content_%282012%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ocean_Heat_Content_%282012%29.png/440px-Ocean_Heat_Content_%282012%29.png 2x" data-file-width="566" data-file-height="381" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ocean_Heat_Content_(2012).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ocean heat content (OHC), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.nodc.noaa.gov/OC5/3M_HEAT_CONTENT/">NOAA</a> 2012</div></div></div>
<p>In terms of heat content, it is the world ocean that dominates the atmospheric climate. The oceans store more than 90% of the heat in Earth's climate system and act as a buffer against the effects of climate change. For instance, an average temperature increase of the entire world ocean by 0.01&#160;°C may seem small, but in fact it represents a very large increase in heat content. If all the heat associated with this anomaly was instantaneously transferred to the entire global atmosphere it would increase the average temperature of the atmosphere by approximately 10<span class="">&#160;</span>°C.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Thus, a small change in the mean temperature of the ocean represents a very large change in the total heat content of the climate system. Of course, when the ocean gains heat the water expands and this represents a component of global sea-level rise.
</p><p>The thermal expansion of water increases with temperature and pressure of the water. Hence, cold <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a> water will expand less for a given increase in temperature compared to warm tropical water. Because different climate models have slightly different patterns of ocean heating, they do not agree fully on the predictions for the contribution of ocean heating on sea level rise.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antarctica">Antarctica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Antarctica">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Antarctic_shelf_ice_hg.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Antarctic_shelf_ice_hg.png/220px-Antarctic_shelf_ice_hg.png" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Antarctic_shelf_ice_hg.png/330px-Antarctic_shelf_ice_hg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Antarctic_shelf_ice_hg.png/440px-Antarctic_shelf_ice_hg.png 2x" data-file-width="2834" data-file-height="1749" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Antarctic_shelf_ice_hg.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Processes around an Antarctic ice shelf</div></div></div>
<p>The large volume of ice on the Antarctic continent stores around 70% of the world's fresh water.<sup id="cite_ref-howstuffworks_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-howstuffworks-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Antarctica#Ice_mass_and_global_sea_level" title="Antarctica">Antarctic ice sheet mass balance</a> is affected by snowfall accumulations, and ice discharge along the periphery. Under the influence of global warming, melt at the base of the ice sheet increases. Simultaneously, the capacity of the atmosphere to carry precipitation increases with temperature so that precipitation, in the form of snowfall, increases. Furthermore, the additional snowfall causes increased ice flow which leads to further loss of ice.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Different satellite methods for measuring ice mass and change are in good agreement, and combining methods leads to more certainty how the <a href="/wiki/East_Antarctic_Ice_Sheet" title="East Antarctic Ice Sheet">East Antarctic Ice Sheet</a>, the <a href="/wiki/West_Antarctic_Ice_Sheet" title="West Antarctic Ice Sheet">West Antarctic Ice Sheet</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Peninsula" title="Antarctic Peninsula">Antarctic Peninsula</a> evolve.<sup id="cite_ref-IMBIE2012_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMBIE2012-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> A 2018 <a href="/wiki/Systematic_review" title="Systematic review">systematic review</a> study estimated that ice loss across the entire continent was 43 gigatons (Gt) per year on average during the period from 1992 to 2002, but has accelerated to an average of 220 Gt per year during the five years from 2012 to 2017. Most of the melt comes from the West Antarctic Ice Sheet, but the Antarctic Peninsula also positively contributes. The East Antarctic Ice Sheet does not contribute much and scientists are not able to determine whether it gains or loses mass.<sup id="cite_ref-IMBIE2018_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMBIE2018-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The sea-level budget from Antarctica has been estimated to be 0.25&#160;mm (0.0098&#160;in) per year from 1993–2005, and 0.42&#160;mm (0.017&#160;in) per year from 2005 to 2015. All datasets generally show an acceleration of mass loss from the Antarctic ice-sheet, but with year-to-year variations.<sup id="cite_ref-WCRP2018_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCRP2018-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Antarctica">East Antarctica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: East Antarctica">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The world's largest potential source of sea level rise is the East Antarctic Ice Sheet, which holds enough ice to raise global sea levels by 53.3 m.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Satellite observations suggest the overall mass balance of the East Antarctic Ice Sheet was relatively steady or slightly positive for much of the period from 1992–2017,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> with the notable exception of <a href="/wiki/Totten_Glacier" title="Totten Glacier">Totten Glacier</a>, which has steadily lost mass in recent decades <sup id="cite_ref-ChenWilson2008_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChenWilson2008-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> in response to ocean warming<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and possibly a reduction in local sea ice cover.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Totten Glacier is the primary outlet of the <a href="/wiki/Aurora_Subglacial_Basin" title="Aurora Subglacial Basin">Aurora Sublgacial Basin</a>, which together with the <a href="/wiki/Wilkes_Basin" title="Wilkes Basin">Wilkes Basin</a> are the two major ice reservoirs in East Antarctica which are subject to potential rapid collapse through <a href="/wiki/Marine_ice_sheet_instability" title="Marine ice sheet instability">marine ice sheet instability</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="West_Antarctica">West Antarctica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: West Antarctica">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>West Antarctica is currently experiencing a net outflow of glacial ice, which will increase global sea level over time. A review of the scientific studies looking at data from 1992 to 2017 suggests an increase in the melt from around 53 ± 29 Gt of ice per year to 159 ± 26 Gt.<sup id="cite_ref-IMBIE2018_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMBIE2018-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Significant acceleration of outflow glaciers in the <a href="/wiki/Amundsen_Sea_Embayment" class="mw-redirect" title="Amundsen Sea Embayment">Amundsen Sea Embayment</a> may have contributed to this increase.<sup id="cite_ref-RignotBamber2008_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RignotBamber2008-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The data showed that the <a href="/wiki/Amundsen_Sea" title="Amundsen Sea">Amundsen Sea</a> sector of the West Antarctic Ice Sheet was discharging 250 cubic kilometres (60&#160;cu&#160;mi) of ice every year, which was 60% more than the precipitation accumulation in the <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">catchment</a> areas. This alone was sufficient to raise the sea level at 0.24&#160;mm (0.0094&#160;in) per year. Further, thinning rates for the glaciers studied in 2002–2003 had increased over the values measured in the early 1990s.
</p><p>Annual temperatures based on <a href="/wiki/Byrd_Station" title="Byrd Station">Byrd Station</a> (central West Antarctica) from 1958 to 2010 increased linear by 2.4 ± 1.2&#160;°C, the study authors note, "West Antarctica as one of the fastest-warming regions globally. In contrast to previous studies, we report statistically significant warming [...] particularly in December–January, the peak of the melting season. A continued rise in summer temperatures could lead to more frequent and extensive episodes of surface melting of the West Antarctic Ice Sheet."<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

data-bandwidth=&quot;768579&quot; data-framerate=&quot;29.97&quot;/&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e9/West_Antarctic_Collapse.ogv/West_Antarctic_Collapse.ogv.160p.webm&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Low bandwidth WebM (160P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 160P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;160p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;284&quot; data-height=&quot;160&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;201768&quot; data-framerate=&quot;29.97&quot;/&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e9/West_Antarctic_Collapse.ogv/West_Antarctic_Collapse.ogv.240p.webm&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Small WebM (240P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 240P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;240p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;426&quot; data-height=&quot;240&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;304752&quot; data-framerate=&quot;29.97&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:West Antarctic Collapse.ogv" style="width:220px;height:124px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/West_Antarctic_Collapse.ogv/220px--West_Antarctic_Collapse.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e9/West_Antarctic_Collapse.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:West_Antarctic_Collapse.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A graphical representation of how warm waters, and the Marine Ice Sheet Instability and Marine Ice Cliff Instability processes are affecting the West Antarctic Ice Sheet</div></div></div>
<p>Two types of instability are at play in West Antarctica. The first one is the <a href="/wiki/Marine_Ice_Sheet_Instability" class="mw-redirect" title="Marine Ice Sheet Instability">Marine Ice Sheet Instability</a>, the bedrock on which parts of the ice sheet rest is moving deeper inland. This means that when a part of the ice sheet melts, a thicker part of the ice sheet is exposed, which may lead to additional ice loss. Secondly, melting of the <a href="/wiki/Ice_shelf" title="Ice shelf">ice shelfs</a>, the floating extensions of the ice sheet, leads to a process named the Marine Ice Cliff Instability<i>.</i> Because they function as a <a href="/wiki/Buttress" title="Buttress">buttress</a> to the ice sheet, their melt leads to additional ice flow. Melt of ice shelfs is accelerated when surface melt creates <a href="/wiki/Crevasse" title="Crevasse">crevasses</a> and these crevasses cause fracturing.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since most of the <a href="/wiki/Bedrock" title="Bedrock">bedrock</a> underlying the West Antarctic Ice Sheet lies well below sea level and ocean waters are warming, the ice sheet is becoming less stable.<sup id="cite_ref-Pollard2015_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pollard2015-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> A rapid collapse of West Antarctic Ice Sheet could raise sea level by 3.3 metres (11&#160;ft) at an unknown rate.<sup id="cite_ref-Bamber2009_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bamber2009-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Thwaites_glacier" class="mw-redirect" title="Thwaites glacier">Thwaites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pine_Island_glacier" class="mw-redirect" title="Pine Island glacier">Pine Island</a> glaciers have been identified to be potentially prone to these processes, since both glaciers bedrock <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a> gets deeper farther inland, exposing them to more warm water intrusion at the grounding line, and with the continued melt, retreat, eventually raising global sea levels.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenland">Greenland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Greenland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Greenland_ssi_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Greenland_ssi_2007.jpg/170px-Greenland_ssi_2007.jpg" width="170" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Greenland_ssi_2007.jpg/255px-Greenland_ssi_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Greenland_ssi_2007.jpg/340px-Greenland_ssi_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="3200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Greenland_ssi_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> 2007 melt anomaly, measured as the difference between the number of days on which melting occurred in 2007 compared to the average annual melting days from 1988–2006<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Most ice on Greenland is part of the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet" title="Greenland ice sheet">Greenland ice sheet</a> which rises to an average of 2.135 kilometres (1.327&#160;mi). The rest of the ice on Greenland is part of isolated glaciers and ice caps.
</p><p>The sources contributing to sea level rise from Greenland are from ice sheet melting (70%) and from glacier calving (30%). Dust, <a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">soot</a>, and microbes and algae living on parts of the ice sheet further enhance melting by darkening its surface and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation#Surface_effects" title="Thermal radiation">thus absorbing more</a> <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">thermal radiation</a>; these regions grew by 12% between 2000 and 2012, and are likely to expand further. Estimates on future contribution to sea level rise from Greenland range from 0.3 to 3 metres (1&#160;ft 0&#160;in to 9&#160;ft 10&#160;in), for the year 2100.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of Greenland's largest outlet glaciers, such as <a href="/wiki/Jakobshavn_Isbr%C3%A6" class="mw-redirect" title="Jakobshavn Isbræ">Jakobshavn Isbræ</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kangerlussuaq_Glacier" title="Kangerlussuaq Glacier">Kangerlussuaq Glacier</a> have seen an acceleration in how fast they are flowing into the ocean.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> It was shown that this acceleration of <a href="/wiki/Outlet_glacier" class="mw-redirect" title="Outlet glacier">outlet glaciers</a> had mostly taken place on the Southern part of Greenland (66 N in 1996), but had spread further north (70 N) in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The contribution of the Greenland ice sheet on sea level over the next couple of centuries can be very high due to a self-reinforcing cycle (a so-called <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a>). After an initial period of melting, the height of the ice sheet will have lowered. As air temperature increases closer to the sea surface, more melt starts to occur. This melting may further be accelerated because the color of ice is darker while it is melting. There is a threshold in surface warming beyond which a partial or near-complete melting of the Greenland ice sheet occurs. Different research has put this threshold value as low as 1.0&#160;°C, and definitely 4.0&#160;°C, above pre-industrial temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ipcc13_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc13-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1170</span></sup>
</p><p>Accounting for the Greenland ice sheet, its peripheral glaciers and ice caps, contributions to current sea level rise have been estimated to be 43%. A study published in 2017, concluded that Greenland’s glaciers and ice caps crossed an irreversible tipping point in 1997, and will continue to melt.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Results based on the existing literature found estimates for the Greenland ice sheet and its glaciers and ice caps, they were the largest contributor to the observed sea level rise from <i>land ice</i> sources (excluding thermal expansion), combined accounting for 71 percent, or 1.32&#160;mm per year during the 2012–2016 period.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glaciers">Glaciers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Glaciers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850" title="Retreat of glaciers since 1850">Retreat of glaciers since 1850</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glacier_mass_balance" title="Glacier mass balance">Glacier mass balance</a></div>
<p>Mountain glaciers include only a minor fraction of all water bound up in glaciers ice (&lt;1%), compared to a bigger portion in Greenland and Antarctica (99%). Still, mountain glaciers have contributed appreciably to historical sea level rise and are set to contribute a smaller, but still significant fraction of sea level rise in the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The roughly 200,000 glaciers on earth are spread out across all continents.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Different glaciers respond differently to increasing temperatures. For instance, valley glaciers that have a shallow slope already retreat under mild warming. Every glacier has a height above which there is net gain in mass and under which the glacier loses mass. If that height changes a bit, this has large consequences for glaciers with a shallow slope.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>345</span></sup> A large set of glaciers drain into the ocean and ice loss can therefore increase when ocean temperatures increase.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Observational and modelling studies of <a href="/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850" title="Retreat of glaciers since 1850">mass loss from glaciers and ice caps</a> indicate a contribution to sea-level rise of 0.2–0.4&#160;mm/yr, averaged over the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Over the 21st century, this is expected to rise, with glaciers contributing 7 to 24 centimetres (3 to 9&#160;in) to global sea levels.<sup id="cite_ref-ipcc13_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc13-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1165</span></sup> Glaciers contributed around 40% to sea-level rise during the 20th century, with estimates for the 21st century of around 30%.<sup id="cite_ref-WCRP2018_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCRP2018-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_ice">Sea ice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Sea ice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sea ice melt has a very small contribution to global sea level rise. According to <a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_principle" title="Archimedes&#39; principle">Archimedes' principle</a>, sea ice that melts does not take up more volume than it had in the form of sea ice or icebergs. However, this only holds true in the case that the salinity of the sea ice and sea water are equal. This assumption is not valid in the case of melting sea ice, where the sea ice contains less salt than sea water. Fresh water has a larger volume compared to salt water, and as such there can be a small contribution of sea ice melt. In the case that all floating <a href="/wiki/Ice_shelf" title="Ice shelf">ice shelves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Icebergs" class="mw-redirect" title="Icebergs">icebergs</a> melt, the sea levels would rise only by about 4&#160;cm (1.6&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-NoerdlingerOthers2007a_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NoerdlingerOthers2007a-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_water_storage">Land water storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Land water storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Trends-in-land-water-storage-from-GRACE-observations,-April-2002-to-November-2014.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Trends-in-land-water-storage-from-GRACE-observations%2C-April-2002-to-November-2014.jpg/220px-Trends-in-land-water-storage-from-GRACE-observations%2C-April-2002-to-November-2014.jpg" width="220" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Trends-in-land-water-storage-from-GRACE-observations%2C-April-2002-to-November-2014.jpg/330px-Trends-in-land-water-storage-from-GRACE-observations%2C-April-2002-to-November-2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Trends-in-land-water-storage-from-GRACE-observations%2C-April-2002-to-November-2014.jpg/440px-Trends-in-land-water-storage-from-GRACE-observations%2C-April-2002-to-November-2014.jpg 2x" data-file-width="599" data-file-height="307" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Trends-in-land-water-storage-from-GRACE-observations,-April-2002-to-November-2014.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Trends in land water storage from <a href="/wiki/Gravity_Recovery_and_Climate_Experiment" title="Gravity Recovery and Climate Experiment">GRACE</a> observations in gigatons per year, April 2002 to November 2014 (glaciers and ice sheets are excluded).</div></div></div>
<p>Humans impact how much water is stored on land. Building <a href="/wiki/Dam" title="Dam">dams</a> prevents large masses of water from flowing into the sea and therefore increases the storage of water on land. On the other hand humans extract water from <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">underground reservoirs</a> for <a href="/wiki/Food_industry" title="Food industry">food production</a> leading to rising seas. Furthermore, the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">hydrological cycle</a> is influenced by climate change and <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, which can lead to further positive and negative contributions to sea level rise. In the 20th century, these processes roughly balanced, but dam building has slowed down and is expected to stay low for the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ipcc13_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc13-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1155</span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Models">Models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are broadly two ways of modelling sea level rise and making future <a href="/wiki/General_circulation_model#Projections" title="General circulation model">projections</a>. On the one hand, scientist use process-based modelling, where all relevant and well-understood physical processes are included in a physical model. An <a href="/wiki/Ice-sheet_model" title="Ice-sheet model">ice-sheet model</a> is used to calculate the contributions of ice sheets and a <a href="/wiki/General_circulation_model" title="General circulation model">general circulation model</a> is used to compute the rising sea temperature and its expansion. A disadvantage of this method is that not all relevant processes might be understood to a sufficient level. Alternatively, some scientist use semi-empirical techniques that use geological data from the past to determine likely sea level responses to a warming world in addition to some basic physical modelling. Semi-empiral modelling relies on sophisticated statistical techniques.<sup id="cite_ref-Mengel_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mengel-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> This type of modelling was partially motivated by the fact that in the 2007 IPCC report, most physical models underestimated the amount of sea level rise compared to observations.<sup id="cite_ref-ipcc13_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc13-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Projections">Projections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Projections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Projected_change_in_global_sea_level_rise_if_atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_were_to_either_quadruple_or_double_(NOAA_GFDL).png" class="image"><img alt="Refer to caption and image description" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Projected_change_in_global_sea_level_rise_if_atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_were_to_either_quadruple_or_double_%28NOAA_GFDL%29.png/220px-Projected_change_in_global_sea_level_rise_if_atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_were_to_either_quadruple_or_double_%28NOAA_GFDL%29.png"
