1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Projected_change_in_global_sea_level_rise_if_atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_were_to_either_quadruple_or_double_%28NOAA_GFDL%29.png/440px-Projected_change_in_global_sea_level_rise_if_atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_were_to_either_quadruple_or_double_%28NOAA_GFDL%29.png 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="485" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Projected_change_in_global_sea_level_rise_if_atmospheric_carbon_dioxide_concentrations_were_to_either_quadruple_or_double_(NOAA_GFDL).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This graph shows the minimum projected change in global sea level rise if <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Atmospheric carbon dioxide">atmospheric carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>) concentrations were to either quadruple or double. <sup id="cite_ref-gfdl_thermal_expansion_projections_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gfdl_thermal_expansion_projections-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The projection is based on several multi-century integrations of a <a href="/wiki/Geophysical_Fluid_Dynamics_Laboratory" title="Geophysical Fluid Dynamics Laboratory">GFDL</a> <a href="/wiki/Global_climate_model" class="mw-redirect" title="Global climate model">global coupled ocean-atmosphere model</a>. These projections are the expected changes due to <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a> of sea water alone, and do not include the effect of melted continental <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">ice sheets</a>. With the effect of ice sheets included the total rise will be larger, by an uncertain but possibly substantial factor.<sup id="cite_ref-gfdl_thermal_expansion_projections_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gfdl_thermal_expansion_projections-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Image credit: <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">NOAA</a> GFDL.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) has made predictions of sea level changes to the year 2100, using the available scientific literature. Their projections are based on the contributors to sea level rise, but do exclude some processes that are less understood. In the case of rapid cuts in emission (the so-called RCP2.6 scenario), the IPCC deem it likely that the sea level will rise to 26–55&#160;cm (10–22&#160;in) with a 67% <a href="/wiki/Confidence_interval" title="Confidence interval">confidence interval</a>. The higher value should thus not be read as an upper limit, which can be substantially higher. For a scenario with very high emissions, the IPCC project the sea level to rise to 52–98&#160;cm (20–39&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-ipcc13_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc13-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Compared to the previous IPCC estimate, more sea level rise is expected for similar scenarios.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Projections assessed by the <a href="/wiki/US_National_Research_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="US National Research Council">US National Research Council</a> (2010)<sup id="cite_ref-nrcpro_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrcpro-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> suggest possible sea level rise over the 21st century of between 56 and 200&#160;cm (22 and 79&#160;in). The NRC describes the IPCC projections as "conservative".<sup id="cite_ref-nrcpro_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrcpro-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In 2011, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Rignot" title="Eric Rignot">Rignot</a> and others projected a rise of 32 centimetres (13&#160;in) by 2050. Their projection included increased contributions from the Antarctic and Greenland ice sheets. Use of two completely different approaches reinforced the Rignot projection.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Romm10Mar2011_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Romm10Mar2011-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Other estimates suggest that for the same period, global mean sea level could rise by 0.2 to 2.0&#160;m (8&#160;in to 6&#160;ft 7&#160;in), relative to the mean sea level in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-parris_sea_level_rise_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parris_sea_level_rise-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Third <a href="/wiki/National_Climate_Assessment" title="National Climate Assessment">National Climate Assessment</a> (NCA), released May 6, 2014, projected a sea level rise of 1 to 4 feet (0.3 to 1&#160;m) by 2100. Decision makers who are particularly susceptible to risk may wish to use a wider range of scenarios from 20 to 200&#160;cm (8 to 80&#160;in) by 2100.<sup id="cite_ref-Sea_Level_Rise_Key_Message_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sea_Level_Rise_Key_Message-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2016 study concluded that based on <a href="/wiki/MIS_5e" class="mw-redirect" title="MIS 5e">past climate change</a> data, sea level rise could accelerate exponentially in the coming decades, with a doubling time of 10, 20 or 40 years, respectively, raising the ocean by several meters, in 50, 100 or 200 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Hansen2016_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen2016-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> However, Greg Holland from the <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Atmospheric_Research" title="National Center for Atmospheric Research">National Center for Atmospheric Research</a>, who reviewed the study, noted: “There is no doubt that the sea level rise, within the IPCC, is a very conservative number, so the truth lies somewhere between IPCC and Jim<i>.</i>”<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One 2017 study's scenario, assuming high <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> use for combustion and strong economic growth during this century, projects sea level rise of up to 1.32 metres (4.3&#160;ft) on average — and an extreme scenario with as much as 1.89 metres (6.2&#160;ft), by 2100. This could mean rapid sea level rise of up to 19 millimeters per year by the end of the century. The study also concluded that the Paris climate agreement emissions scenario, if met, would result in a median 0.52 metres (1.7&#160;ft) of sea level rise by 2100.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Estimates of future sea level were also produced in the 20th century. For instance, Hansen et al. 1981, published the study <i>Climate impact of increasing atmospheric carbon dioxide</i>, and predicted that anthropogenic carbon dioxide warming and its potential effects on climate in the 21st century could cause a sea level rise of 5 to 6&#160;m (16 to 20&#160;ft), from melting of the West Antarctic ice-sheet alone.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_2100">After 2100</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: After 2100">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Long-term_effects_of_global_warming" title="Long-term effects of global warming">Long-term effects of global warming</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg/220px-6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg/330px-6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg/440px-6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Earth with a long-term 6-metre (20&#160;ft) sea level rise represented in red (uniform distribution, actual sea level rise will vary regionally).</div></div></div>
<p>There is a widespread consensus that substantial long-term sea-level rise will continue for centuries to come even if the temperature stabilizes.<sup id="cite_ref-nrccon_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrccon-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> IPCC <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fourth Assessment Report">AR4</a> estimated that at least a partial deglaciation of the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet" title="Greenland ice sheet">Greenland ice sheet</a>, and possibly the <a href="/wiki/West_Antarctic_ice_sheet" class="mw-redirect" title="West Antarctic ice sheet">West Antarctic ice sheet</a>, would occur given a global average temperature increase of 1–4&#160;°C  (relative to temperatures over the years 1990–2000).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> This estimate was given about a 50% chance of being correct.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The estimated timescale was centuries to millennia, and would contribute 4 to 6 metres (13 to 20&#160;ft) or more to sea levels over this period.
</p><p>Melting of the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet" title="Greenland ice sheet">Greenland ice sheet</a> could contribute an additional 4 to 7.5&#160;m (10 to 20&#160;ft) over many thousands of years.<sup id="cite_ref-us_nrc_2011_long_term_slr_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_2011_long_term_slr-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It has been estimated that we are already committed to a sea-level rise of approximately 2.3&#160;m (7&#160;ft 7&#160;in) for each degree of temperature rise within the next 2,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Warming beyond the 2&#160;°C target would potentially lead to rates of sea-level rise dominated by ice loss from <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>. Continued carbon dioxide emissions from fossil fuel sources could cause additional tens of metres of sea level rise, over the next millennia, and ultimately melt the entire Antarctic ice sheet, causing about 58&#160;m (190&#160;ft) of sea level rise.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After 500 years, sea-level rise from thermal expansion alone may have reached only half of its eventual level, which models suggest may lie within ranges of 0.5 to 2&#160;m (1&#160;ft 8&#160;in to 6&#160;ft 7&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-solomon_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-solomon-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Rising sea levels will cause flooding and may erase entire cities. In a study published by <a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)"><i>Nature</i></a>, the entire state of <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a> could be completely wiped out by 2500.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_sea_level_rise">Regional sea level rise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Regional sea level rise">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Subsidence" title="Subsidence">Subsidence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Post-glacial_rebound" title="Post-glacial rebound">isostatic rebound</a>, gravitational effects of changing ice masses, and spatially varying patterns of warming lead to differences in sea level rise around the globe.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many ports, urban conglomerations, and agricultural regions are built on <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">river deltas</a>, where subsidence of land contributes to a substantially increased <i>effective</i> sea level rise.  This is caused by both unsustainable extraction of groundwater (in some places also by extraction of oil and gas), and by <a href="/wiki/Levee" title="Levee">levees</a> and other flood management practices that prevent accumulation of sediments from compensating for the natural settling of deltaic soils.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In many deltas, this results in subsidence ranging from several millimeters per year up to possibly 25 centimeters per year in parts of the Ciliwung delta (<a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Total anthropogenic-caused subsidence in the <a href="/wiki/Rhine-Meuse-Scheldt_delta" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhine-Meuse-Scheldt delta">Rhine-Meuse-Scheldt delta</a> (Netherlands) is estimated at 3 to 4&#160;m (9.8 to 13.1&#160;ft), over 3&#160;m (9.8&#160;ft) in urban areas of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River_Delta" title="Mississippi River Delta">Mississippi River Delta</a> (<a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>), and over 9&#160;m (30&#160;ft) in the <a href="/wiki/Sacramento-San_Joaquin_River_Delta" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacramento-San Joaquin River Delta">Sacramento-San Joaquin River Delta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Atlantic is set to warm at a faster pace than the Pacific. This has consequences for Europe and the <a href="/wiki/U.S._East_Coast" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. East Coast">U.S. East Coast</a>, which may receive a sea level rise 3–4 times the global average.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The downturn of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_meridional_overturning_circulation" title="Atlantic meridional overturning circulation">Atlantic meridional overturning circulation</a> (AMOC) has been also tied to extreme regional sea level rise on the US Northeast Coast.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Regional_effects_of_global_warming" title="Regional effects of global warming">Regional effects of global warming</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Major_cities_threatened_by_sea_level_rise.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Major_cities_threatened_by_sea_level_rise.png/220px-Major_cities_threatened_by_sea_level_rise.png" width="220" height="96" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Major_cities_threatened_by_sea_level_rise.png/330px-Major_cities_threatened_by_sea_level_rise.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Major_cities_threatened_by_sea_level_rise.png/440px-Major_cities_threatened_by_sea_level_rise.png 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="625" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Major_cities_threatened_by_sea_level_rise.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of major cities of the world most vulnerable to sea level rise</div></div></div>
<p>Current and future climate change is set to have a number of impacts, particularly on <a href="/wiki/Coast" title="Coast">coastal</a> systems. Such impacts include increased <a href="/wiki/Coastal_erosion" title="Coastal erosion">coastal erosion</a>, higher <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm-surge</a> flooding, inhibition of <a href="/wiki/Primary_production" title="Primary production">primary production</a> processes, more extensive coastal inundation, changes in surface <a href="/wiki/Water_quality" title="Water quality">water quality</a> and groundwater characteristics, increased loss of property and coastal habitats, increased flood risk and potential loss of life, loss of non-monetary cultural resources and values, impacts on agriculture and <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a> through decline in soil and water quality, and loss of tourism, recreation, and transportation functions.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>356</span></sup>
</p><p>Many of these impacts are detrimental — especially for the three-quarters of the world's poor who depend on agriculture systems.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The report does, however, note that owing to the great diversity of coastal environments; regional and local differences in projected relative sea level and climate changes; and differences in the resilience and adaptive capacity of <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, sectors, and countries, the impacts will be highly variable in time and space. <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">River deltas</a> and small island states are particularly vulnerable to sea-level rise.
</p><p>Globally tens of millions of people will be displaced in the latter decades of the century if greenhouse gases are not reduced drastically. Many coastal areas have large population growth, which results in more people at risk from sea level rise. The rising seas pose both a direct risk: unprotected homes can be flooded, and indirect threats of higher storm surges, tsunamis and king tides. Asia has the largest population at risk from sea level with countries such as <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> having very densely populated coastal areas.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The effects of displacement are very dependent on how successful governments will be in implementing defenses against the rising sea, with concerns for the poorerst countries such as sub-Saharan countries and island nations.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coastal_areas">Coastal areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Coastal areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ten per cent of the world's population live in coastal areas that are less than 10 metres (33&#160;ft) above sea level. Furthermore, two thirds of the world's cities with over five million people are located in these low-lying coastal areas.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Future sea level rise could lead to potentially catastrophic difficulties for shore-based communities in the next centuries: for example, many major cities such as <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> already need storm-surge defenses, and will need more if the sea level rises; they also face issues such as <a href="/wiki/Subsidence" title="Subsidence">subsidence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Egyptian city <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> faces a similar situation, where hundreds of thousands people living in the low-lying areas may already have to be relocated in the coming decade. The nearby farmland in the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a> is also affected by salt water flooding.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> However, modest increases in sea level are likely to be offset when cities adapt by constructing sea walls or through relocating.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The impacts of sea level rise are strong in the eastern coast of <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">Miami</a> has been listed as "the number-one most vulnerable city worldwide" in terms of potential damage to property from storm-related flooding and sea-level rise.<sup id="cite_ref-RS62013_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RS62013-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> According to a 2011 study conducted by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Geological_Survey" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Geological Survey">U.S. Geological Survey</a>, 68 percent of beaches in <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> and the mid-Atlantic states observe <a href="/wiki/Coastal_erosion" title="Coastal erosion">coastal erosion</a>, with some barrier beaches in <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> recording twenty or more meters of eroding coastlines per year. However, the rate of coastal erosion is partially related to human developments, eg, bulldozing dunes.<sup id="cite_ref-ClimateCentral2012_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClimateCentral2012-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Rising seas has also been tied to an increased risk from <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunamis</a>, potentially affecting coastal cities in the Pacific and Atlantic Oceans.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Island_nations">Island nations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Island nations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Alliance_of_Small_Island_States" title="Alliance of Small Island States">Alliance of Small Island States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Small_Island_Developing_States" title="Small Island Developing States">Small Island Developing States</a></div><p><a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">Atolls</a> and low-lying coastal areas on islands are particularly vulnerable to sea level rise. Possible impacts include <a href="/wiki/Coastal_erosion" title="Coastal erosion">coastal erosion</a>, floodings and salt intrusion <a href="/wiki/Soil_salinization" class="mw-redirect" title="Soil salinization">into soils</a> and freshwater. It is difficult to assess how much of past erosion and floods have been caused by sea level change, compared to other environmental events such as hurricanes. Adaptation to sea level rise is costly for small island nation as a large portion of their population lives in areas that are at risk.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TWSeaLevelRise1.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/TWSeaLevelRise1.gif/220px-TWSeaLevelRise1.gif" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/TWSeaLevelRise1.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TWSeaLevelRise1.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic animation of projected sea level rise around <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu" title="Tuvalu">Tuvalu</a>, and other low-lying countries are among the areas that are at the highest level of risk. At current rates, sea level would be high enough to make the Maldives uninhabitable by 2100.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Geomorphology" title="Geomorphology">Geomorphological</a> events such as storms tend to have larger impacts on reef island than sea level rise, for instance at one of the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Islands" title="Marshall Islands">Marshall Islands</a>. These effects include the immediate erosion and subsequent regrowth process that may vary in length from decades to centuries, even resulting in land areas larger than pre-storm values. With an expected rise in the frequency and intensity of storms, they may become more significant in determining island shape and size than sea level rise.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Five of the <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">Solomon Islands</a> have disappeared due to the combined effects of sea level rise and stronger trade winds that were pushing water into the Western Pacific.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the case all islands of an island nation become uninhabitable or completely submerged by the sea, the states themselves would also become dissolved. Once this happens, all rights on the surrounding area (sea) are removed. This area can be huge as rights extend to a radius of 224 nautical miles (415&#160;km; 258&#160;mi) around the entire island state. Any resources, such as fossil oil, minerals and metals, within this area can be freely dug up by anyone and sold without needing to pay any commission to the (now dissolved) island state.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecosystems">Ecosystems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Ecosystems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Coastal ecosystems are facing drastic changes as a consequence of rising sea levels. Many systems might ultimately be lost when sea levels rise too much or too fast. Some ecosystems can move land inward with the high-water mark, but many are prevented from migrating due to natural or man-made barriers. This 'coastal squeeze' could result in the loss of habitats such as <a href="/wiki/Mudflat" title="Mudflat">mudflats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Human activities, such as dam building, may restrict sediment supplies to wetlands, and thereby prevent natural adaptation processes. The loss of some <a href="/wiki/Tidal_marsh" title="Tidal marsh">tidal marshes</a> is unavoidable as a consequence.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mangroves" class="mw-redirect" title="Mangroves">Mangroves</a>, among the most widely studied ecosystems in the world, adjust to rising sea levels by building vertically using accumulated <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organic_matter" title="Organic matter">organic matter</a>. If sea level rise is too rapid, they will not be able to keep up and will instead be submerged.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> As mangroves protect against storm surges, waves and tsunamis, losing them makes the effects of sea level rise worse.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When seawater approaches inland, problems related to contaminated soils and flooded wetlands may occur. Also, fish, birds, and coastal plants could lose parts of their habitat.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Coral, important for bird and fish life, needs to grow vertically to remain close to the sea surface in order to get enough energy from sunlight. It has so far been able to keep up the vertical growth with the rising seas, but might not be able to do so in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, it was reported that the <a href="/wiki/Bramble_Cay_melomys" title="Bramble Cay melomys">Bramble Cay melomys</a>, which lived on a <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Great Barrier Reef">Great Barrier Reef</a> island, had probably become extinct because of inundation due to sea level rises.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adaptation">Adaptation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_level_rise&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Adaptation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Adaptation_to_global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Adaptation to global warming">Adaptation to global warming</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beach_restoration_device.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Beach_restoration_device.jpg/300px-Beach_restoration_device.jpg" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Beach_restoration_device.jpg/450px-Beach_restoration_device.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Beach_restoration_device.jpg/600px-Beach_restoration_device.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2016" data-file-height="1512" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beach_restoration_device.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beach nourishment in progress in <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Adaptation options to sea level rise can be broadly classified into <i>retreat, accommodate</i> and <i>protect</i>. Retreating is moving people and <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a> to less exposed areas and preventing further development in areas that are at risk. This type of adaptation is potentially disruptive, as displacement of people might lead to tensions. Accommodation options are measurements that make societies more flexible to sea level rise. Examples are the <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">cultivation</a> of food crops that tolerate a high salt content in the soil and making new <a href="/wiki/Building_code" title="Building code">building standards</a> which require building to be built higher and have less damage in the case a flood does occur. Finally, areas can be protected by the construction of dams, dikes and by improving natural defenses.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These adaptation options can be further divided into <i>hard</i> and <i>soft</i>. Hard adaptation relies mostly on capital-intensive human-built infrastructure and involves large-scale changes to human societies and ecological systems. Because of its large scale, it is often not flexible. Soft adaptation involves strengthening natural defenses and adaptation strategies in local communities and the use of simple and modular technology, which can be locally owned. The two types of adaptation might be complementary or mutually exclusive. The building of a dike (hard adaptation) for instance destroys the natural dune system and dune nourishment will not be possible anymore.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many countries are developing concrete plans for adaptation. An example is the extension of the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Dutch</a> <a href="/wiki/Delta_Works" title="Delta Works">Delta Works</a>. In 2008, the Dutch <i>Delta Commission</i>, advised in a report that the Netherlands would need a massive new building program to strengthen the country's water defenses against the anticipated <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">effects of global warming</a> for the next 190 years. This included drawing up worst-case plans for evacuations. The plan also included more than €100 billion (US$113 billion) in new spending through to the year 2100 to implement precautionary measures, such as broadening coastal <a href="/wiki/Dunes" class="mw-redirect" title="Dunes">dunes</a> and strengthening sea and river <a href="/wiki/Dyke_(construction)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dyke (construction)">dikes</a>. The commission said the country must plan for a rise in the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> up to 1.3 metres (4&#160;ft 3&#160;in) by 2100 and plan for a 2-4 m rise by 2200.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> About a quarter of the Netherlands lies beneath sea level, while more than 50% of the nation's area would be inundated by tidal floods if it did not have an <a href="/wiki/Flood_control_in_the_Netherlands" title="Flood control in the Netherlands">extensive levee system</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="New York City Panel on Climate Change">New York City Panel on Climate Change</a> (NPCC) is an effort to prepare the New York City area for climate change. <a href="/wiki/Miami_Beach" class="mw-redirect" title="Miami Beach">Miami Beach</a> is spending $500 million from 2015 to 2020 to address sea-level rise. Actions include a pump drainage system, and raising of roadways and sidewalks.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> U.S. coastal cities also conduct so called <i>beach nourishment</i>, also known as <i>beach replenishment</i>, where new beach sand is trucked in and added.<sup id="cite_ref-ClimateCentral2012_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClimateCentral2012-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Options that have been proposed to assist island nations to <a href="/wiki/Adaptation_to_global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Adaptation to global warming">adapt</a> to rising sea level include abandoning islands, building dikes, and "building upwards."<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Karl, TR;  et al., eds. (2009). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.globalchange.gov/publications/reports/scientific-assessments/us-impacts/"><i>Global Climate Change Impacts in the United States</i></a>. 32 Avenue of the Americas, New York, NY 10013-2473, USA: Cambridge University Press. pp.&#160;22–24. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-521-14407-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-521-14407-0">978-0-521-14407-0</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-nrcpro-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-nrcpro_63-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-nrcpro_63-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">America's Climate Choices: Panel on Advancing the Science of Climate Change, Board on Atmospheric Sciences and Climate, Division on Earth and Life Studies, NATIONAL RESEARCH COUNCIL OF THE NATIONAL ACADEMIES (2010). "7 Sea Level Rise and the Coastal Environment". <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://books.nap.edu/openbook.php?record_id=12782&amp;page=243"><i>Advancing the Science of Climate Change</i></a>. Washington, D.C.: The National Academies Press. pp.&#160;243–250. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-309-14588-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-309-14588-6">978-0-309-14588-6</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2011-06-17</span></span>. <q>(From pg 250) Even if sea-level rise were to remain in the conservative range projected by the IPCC (0.6–1.9 feet [0.18–0.59 m])—not considering potentially much larger increases due to rapid decay of the Greenland or West Antarctic ice sheets—tens of millions of people worldwide would become vulnerable to flooding due to sea-level rise over the next 50 years (Nicholls, 2004; Nicholls and Tol, 2006). This is especially true in densely populated, low-lying areas with limited ability to erect or establish protective measures. In the United States, the high end of the conservative IPCC estimate would result in the loss of a large portion of the nation's remaining coastal wetlands. The impact on the east and Gulf coasts of the United States of 3.3 feet (1 m) of sea-level rise, which is well within the range of more recent projections for the 21st century (e.g., Pfeffer et al., 2008; Vermeer and Rahmstorf, 2009), is shown in pink in <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://books.nap.edu/openbook.php?record_id=12782&amp;page=251#p2001c3c59960251001">Figure 7.7</a>. Also shown, in red, is the effect of 19.8 feet (6 m) of sea-level rise, which could occur over the next several centuries if warming were to continue unabated.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=7+Sea+Level+Rise+and+the+Coastal+Environment&amp;rft.btitle=Advancing+the+Science+of+Climate+Change&amp;rft.place=Washington%2C+D.C.&amp;rft.pages=243-250&amp;rft.pub=The+National+Academies+Press&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-309-14588-6&amp;rft.au=America%27s+Climate+Choices%3A+Panel+on+Advancing+the+Science+of+Climate+Change%2C+Board+on+Atmospheric+Sciences+and+Climate%2C+Division+on+Earth+and+Life+Studies%2C+NATIONAL+RESEARCH+COUNCIL+OF+THE+NATIONAL+ACADEMIES&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fbooks.nap.edu%2Fopenbook.php%3Frecord_id%3D12782%26page%3D243&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASea+level+rise" class="Z3988"></span><span class="citation-comment" style="display:none; color:#33aa33; margin-left:0.3em">CS1 maint: Multiple names: authors list (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_Multiple_names:_authors_list" title="Category:CS1 maint: Multiple names: authors list">link</a>) </span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFRignot_E.I._VelicognaM._R._van_den_BroekeA._Monaghan2011" class="citation journal">Rignot E.; I. Velicogna; M. R. van den Broeke; A. Monaghan; J. Lenaerts (2011). "Acceleration of the contribution of the Greenland and Antarctic ice sheets to sea level rise". <i>Geophysical Research Letters</i>. <b>38</b> (5): n/a. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2011GeoRL..3805503R">2011GeoRL..3805503R</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1029%2F2011GL046583">10.1029/2011GL046583</a>. <q>Considerable disparity remains between these estimates due to the inherent uncertainties of each method, the lack of detailed comparison between independent estimates, and the effect of temporal modulations in ice sheet surface mass balance. Here, we present a consistent record of mass balance for the Greenland and Antarctic ice sheets over the past two decades, validated by the comparison of two independent techniques over the past eight years: one differencing perimeter loss from net accumulation, and one using a dense time series of timevariable gravity. We find excellent agreement between the two techniques for absolute mass loss and acceleration of mass loss.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Geophysical+Research+Letters&amp;rft.atitle=Acceleration+of+the+contribution+of+the+Greenland+and+Antarctic+ice+sheets+to+sea+level+rise&amp;rft.volume=38&amp;rft.issue=5&amp;rft.pages=n%2Fa&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1029%2F2011GL046583&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2011GeoRL..3805503R&amp;rft.au=Rignot+E.&amp;rft.au=I.+Velicogna&amp;rft.au=M.+R.+van+den+Broeke&amp;rft.au=A.+Monaghan&amp;rft.au=J.+Lenaerts&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASea+level+rise" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>
</li>
